Let's define the set A = {positive integers with at least three proper divisors}. For N in A, we compute f(N). We need to determine when f(N) is also in A, and so on.

We can do some reasoning: f(N) = sum of three largest proper divisors. For N in A, what are possible values of f(N)? It can be less than, equal to, or greater than N. We observed that if N is divisible by 6 but not by 4 or 5, then f(N)=N. If N is divisible by 6 and also by 4, then f(N) > N. If N is divisible by 6 and by 5, then f(N) > N. Thus the only way to get an infinite sequence is to eventually hit a fixed point. So the set of possible a_1 is the set of all positive integers N such that the orbit under f never hits a number with <3 proper divisors, i.e., stays within A. Since we have seen that all numbers that are not fixed points eventually either map to a prime or to a square of prime (which is not in A) after some steps, we need to prove that the only numbers that can have an infinite orbit are those that eventually map to a fixed point. And we need to characterize all such N. That is, find all N for which iterating f never leaves A. Since the dynamics is deterministic, we can try to show that if N is not a multiple of 6, then it eventually leads to a prime or a square of prime (i.e., bad). And if N is a multiple of 6, then it either is a fixed point (if not divisible by 4 or 5) or maps to a number that is a fixed point (maybe after some steps). But we saw that 72 maps to 78, which is fixed. Also, 96 maps to 104 then 91 then 21 then 11 stop, so 96 fails. So not all multiples of 6 work. We need to determine exactly which multiples of 6 yield infinite orbit.

Let's analyze the recurrence more systematically.

We have f(N) = N * (1/e1+1/e2+1/e3). Since e1, e2, e3 are the three smallest divisors of N greater than 1.

Let's denote d1 = e1, d2 = e2, d3 = e3. Then d1 | N, d2 | N, d3 | N, and d1 < d2 < d3. Also, note that N/d1, N/d2, N/d3 are the three largest proper divisors (since the mapping is order-reversing). So f(N) = N/d1 + N/d2 + N/d3.

Now, observe that if N is divisible by 6, then d1=2, d2=3, d3 could be 6 or something else if N is divisible by 4 or 5. So the behavior depends on the smallest divisors.

Let's define the set of allowed numbers A. We want to find N such that f(N) ∈ A, and then f(f(N)) ∈ A, etc.

We can try to characterize the "basin of attraction" of the fixed points.

First, note that fixed points are numbers N such that d1=2, d2=3, d3=6. That is, N is divisible by 2 and 3, and not divisible by 4 or 5. So N ≡ 0 mod 6, and N mod 4 ≠ 0, N mod 5 ≠ 0. This set includes numbers like 6, 18, 42, 54, 66, 78, 102, 114, 126, 138, 150? No 150 is divisible by 5, so not. 162? 162=2*3^4, not divisible by 4 or 5? 162/4=40.5, 162/5=32.4, so yes, fixed. So many.

Now, consider numbers that are not divisible by 6. Then we need to see if they can ever produce an infinite orbit. For N odd, we saw they map to a prime or square of prime in at most 2 steps. For N even but not divisible by 3? Then N is even and not multiple of 3. Then d1=2. The next smallest divisor d2 is either 4 if N divisible by 4, or a prime p (odd) if N not divisible by 4. Then d3 is either N itself (if d2 is the only other divisor besides 2 and N? Actually if N=2 * p, then d2=p, d3=N? But N itself is a divisor >1, and it's the largest divisor, so d3 = N (since N has only divisors 2, p, 2p). So f(N) = N/2 + N/p + N/(2p) = N*(1/2+1/p+1/(2p)). This is an integer, and it's less than N for p>3? For p=5, f=8; p=7, f=10; p=11, f=14; p=13, f=16; etc. These f(N) are even? 8,10,14,16,... They are all even except? 8,10,14,16 are even. So f(N) is even. Also, note that f(N) is divisible by 2? Yes. And f(N) might be divisible by 3? For N=10, f=8 not divisible by 3. For N=14, f=10 not divisible by 3. For N=22, f=14 not divisible by 3. For N=26, f=16 not divisible by 3. So f(N) is even but not necessarily multiple of 3. Could f(N) be a fixed point? For f(N) to be fixed, we need f(N) ≡ 0 mod 6 and not divisible by 4 or 5. For example, 8 is even, but 8 mod 6 =2, not 0. So not fixed. 10 mod 6=4. 14 mod 6=2. 16 mod 6=4. So none are multiples of 6. So f(N) is not a fixed point. Then what is f(f(N))? Since f(N) is even and not multiple of 3, we are in the same case: even not divisible by 3. So we can compute f(f(N)) again. For example, N=10: f(10)=8; f(8)=7 (prime) stop. N=14: f(14)=10; f(10)=8; f(8)=7 stop. N=22: f(22)=14; f(14)=10; f(10)=8; f(8)=7 stop. N=26: f(26)=16; f(16)=14; then 10,8,7. So all these eventually hit 7. But is 7 always the final? Could there be a chain that leads to a fixed point? For N even not divisible by 3, we get f(N) = N*(1/2+1/p+1/(2p)) where p is the smallest odd divisor (if N not divisible by 4) or if N divisible by 4? Let's analyze that case: N even and divisible by 4 but not by 3. Then d1=2, d2=4, d3 is the next smallest divisor. If N=4*? Let's compute f(N) for N=20,28,44,52,68,76,92, etc. They gave 19,25,37,43,55,61,73. These are odd numbers that are either prime or product of two primes? 55=5*11, 55's f=17 prime. So these eventually go to primes. Could any of these be a fixed point? For f(N) to be fixed, we need f(N) ≡ 0 mod 6 and not divisible by 4 or 5. The numbers we got: 19,25,37,43,55,61,73. None are multiples of 6. So they are not fixed. Then f(f(N)) will be odd (since f(N) is odd), and then as we saw odd numbers eventually hit a prime or square of prime. So these chains also end in a prime. So it appears that if N is not divisible by 6, then the orbit eventually reaches a prime (or square of prime) and stops. But we need to confirm that there is no cycle that avoids primes. For example, could there be a number N such that f(N) = M and f(M) = N, with both having at least 3 proper divisors? We should check possibility of 2-cycle.

Let's attempt to find possible cycles analytically.

Suppose N and M are such that f(N)=M, f(M)=N, with N≠M. Since f(N) is sum of three largest proper divisors of N, we can try to characterize the possible values.

Note that if N is even, then f(N) is at most something? For even N, d1=2. Then f(N) = N/2 + N/d2 + N/d3. Since d2 and d3 are divisors >2, and at least 3 and 4? Actually d2≥3, d3≥4? So if N is even and not divisible by 3, f(N) is less than N. So if we have a 2-cycle, then one of them must be larger than the other. Suppose N > M. Then f(N)=M, so M < N. Then f(M)=N, so N < M? That would imply N < M and N > M contradiction. So for a 2-cycle, if both are even and not divisible by 3, we get f(N) < N, so M < N, then f(M) < M, so N < M contradiction. So if both are even and not divisible by 3, cannot have 2-cycle. If one is even and the other is odd? Then f(N) could be odd if N is even? For N even, f(N) can be odd? Example: N=20, f=19 odd. So N < M. Then we have N < M and f(N)=M, but f(N) < N (since N even not divisible by 3) so M < N contradiction. So no 2-cycle with N even not divisible by 3 and M odd.

Thus from these examples, it seems that the only way to get an infinite orbit is to eventually land on a fixed point. And the fixed points are exactly numbers that are multiples of 6 but not multiples of 4 or 5. So the set of possible a_1 is the set of all positive integers N such that the orbit under f eventually hits a number that is a multiple of 6 but not a multiple of 4 or 5. And also, all intermediate terms must have at least 3 proper divisors (i.e., be in A). So we need to characterize the "basin of attraction" of the fixed points.

Now, note that the fixed points are isolated in the sense that only themselves map to themselves. But they might have preimages that map to them. For example, we found that 72 maps to 78 (fixed). That term will be of the form (13/12)^k N. For it to be an integer, N must be a multiple of 12^k. So if N is a multiple of 12^k, then the term after k steps is 13^k * (N/12^k). That term may not be a multiple of 12. Then we need to compute f for that term. The orbit will then be determined by the dynamics on numbers that are not multiples of 12. So for the orbit to be infinite, we need that the process never hits a prime or a square of a prime. So we need to understand the structure of the "preimage tree" of the fixed points under the map f.

Now, note that the map for numbers that are multiples of 12 is N -> 13N/12. So if we define M = N/12, then the map is M -> 13M. Actually let's think differently.

Define a sequence: a1 = 12 * m1, then a2 = f(12 m1) = 13 m1. Now, if 13 m1 is a multiple of 12, then we can write a2 = 12 * m2, where m2 = (13 m1)/12. Then a3 = f(12 m2) = 13 m2 = 13*(13 m1/12) = 13^2 m1/12. Then if a3 is a multiple of 12, we have m3 = a3/12 = 13^2 m1/12^2. So we see that if we define m_n = a_{2n+1} / 12? Actually from the pattern, we have a1 = 12 m1, a2 = 13 m1. Then if a2 is a multiple of 12, we have a2 = 12 m2 with m2 = 13 m1 / 12. Then a3 = f(12 m2) = 13 m2 = 13*(13 m1/12) = 13^2 m1/12. Then if a3 is a multiple of 12, we have a3 = 12 m3 with m3 = 13^2 m1/12^2. So in general, if the process continues, we get that the terms a_{2n+1} = 12 * (13^n m1 / 12^n). For this to be an integer, we need 12^n | 13^n m1. Since 13 and 12 are coprime, this is equivalent to 12^n | m1. So if m1 is a multiple of 12^n, then the terms a_{2n+1} are multiples of 12 and the process can continue. So the sequence of M values (M_n = m_n) is: M_0 = m1, M_1 = 13 m1 / 12, M_2 = 13^2 m1 / 12^2, ... So if m1 is a multiple of 12^t, then we can iterate t steps to get a term that is a multiple of 12, and then the next term is 13^t m1 / 12^t. At that point, we have a term a_{2t+1} = 12 * (13^t m1 / 12^t). That term is a multiple of 12 if and only if 12^t | m1. So we can define the "height" as the largest t such that 12^t divides m1. Then after t steps, we get a term a_{2t+1} = 12 * (13^t m1 / 12^t). Now, this term is a multiple of 12, but its "M" value is 13^t m1 / 12^t, which is not divisible by 12 (since we took the maximum t). So we have a term of the form 12 * M', where M' is not divisible by 12. Then the next term a_{2t+2} = 13 M'. Now, we need to analyze the behavior of 13 M' where M' is not divisible by 12. Note that M' may be divisible by 6? Since m1 was a multiple of 6? Wait, m1 is the factor such that N=12m1 is in A. For N to be in A, it must be a multiple of 6? Actually any multiple of 12 is a multiple of 6, so m1 can be any integer. So M' = 13^t m1 / 12^t. Since we took the maximum t such that 12^t | m1, we have that M' is not divisible by 12. But could M' be divisible by 6? Possibly, if m1 had a factor of 6? But m1 may have factors of 2 and 3. Since 12=2^2*3, the condition for 12^t | m1 means that m1 is divisible by 2^{2t} * 3^t. So m1 has at least t factors of 2 and t factors of 3. Then M' = 13^t m1 / 12^t = 13^t * (m1 / 12^t). Since m1 / 12^t is an integer not divisible by 12, it may still be divisible by 2 or 3? From our earlier derivation, the numbers that work are exactly those for which the maximal power of 12 dividing a1/12 is such that the remaining factor (6w) has w odd and not divisible by 5. In other words, let a1 = 12 * m. Then let t = max{k: 12^k | m}. Then we require that m/12^t is of the form 2 * 3 * w (i.e., 6w) with w odd and not divisible by 5. This is equivalent to saying that the odd part of m/12^t is a multiple of 3 but not 5. Since m/12^t is not divisible by 12, its 2-adic exponent is either 0 or 1. If it is 0, then m/12^t is odd, and we need it to be a multiple of 3 and not 5. But then a1 = 12 * 12^t * odd = 12^{t+1} * odd. Then v2(a1)=2(t+1), which is even. So these numbers would have even v2. But we already argued that all working numbers have odd v2. So m/12^t must have exactly one factor 2. That is, m/12^t is even but not divisible by 4. So its 2-adic exponent is 1. Then m/12^t = 2 * (odd). For it to be of the form 6w, we need the odd part to be divisible by 3. So m/12^t = 2 * 3 * w = 6w, with w odd and not divisible by 5. So the condition is: let a1 = 12 * m. Let t = max{k: 12^k | m}. Then we require that m/12^t is even and divisible by 3 but not by 5. In other words, m/12^t = 6w, with w odd and 5∤w. This is the condition for the orbit to be infinite. But note that if a1 is a fixed point, then a1 is not necessarily of the form 12m? Actually a1 is multiple of 6, but if v2=1, then a1 = 2 * 3 * c = 6c. Proof outline:

1. Define f(N) = sum of three largest proper divisors of N.
2. Show that if N has <3 proper divisors, then the sequence cannot be infinite. So a1 must have at least 3 proper divisors.
3. Show that if a1 is not a multiple of 6, then the sequence eventually hits a number with <3 proper divisors. 5. Let a1 = 6c. Then we consider two cases based on whether a1 is divisible by 4.

Better: We can iterate the process. From a1 = 12m, we get a2 = 13m. Now, if a2 is a multiple of 12, then we can apply the same transformation: a2 = 12 * (13m/12). - a1 = 6c, where c is an odd integer not divisible by 5; or

Now, write in XML.</think><solution>
Let \(f(N)\) denote the sum of the three largest proper divisors of \(N\).  
The sequence \((a_n)\) satisfies \(a_{n+1}=f(a_n)\) and each term has at least three proper divisors.  
We determine all positive integers \(a_1\) for which such an infinite sequence exists.

---
### 1.  If \(a_1\) is not a multiple of \(6\) then the sequence cannot be infinite  

Write \(a_1=N\).  

*If \(N\) is odd* and has at least three proper divisors, then its smallest prime divisor \(p\ge 3\).  
The three smallest divisors \(>1\) are at least \(3,5,7\); in any case  
\[
\frac1d_1+\frac1d_2+\frac1d_3\le\frac13+\frac15+\frac17=\frac{71}{105}<1,
\]  
so \(f(N)=N\!\left(\frac1{d_1}+\frac1{d_2}+\frac1{d_3}\right)<N\).  
Thus the sequence is strictly decreasing.  Because it consists of positive integers it must eventually reach a number with fewer than three proper divisors (a prime or a square of a prime), at which point the next term is not defined.  Hence no infinite sequence exists.

*If \(N\) is even but not divisible by \(3\)*, let \(N=2^e\cdot o\) with \(o\) odd and \(e\ge1\).  
Since \(3\nmid N\), we have \(3\nmid o\).  The smallest divisor is \(2\).  
- If \(e=1\) then the three smallest divisors are \(2,p,2p\) where \(p\) is the smallest odd prime factor (\(p\ge5\)).  
  Then \(f(N)=\frac{N}{2}+\frac{N}{p}+\frac{N}{2p}=p+2+1=p+3\le p+3\le p+3\le\frac{N}{2}+\frac{N}{5}+\frac{N}{10}<N\).
- If \(e\ge2\) then \(4\mid N\).  The three smallest divisors are \(2,4,p\) with \(p\ge5\) (because \(4< p\) when \(p>4\); if \(p=3\) then \(3\mid N\), contradicting the assumption).  
  Then \(f(N)=\frac{N}{2}+\frac{N}{4}+\frac{N}{p}=2^{e-1}o+2^{e-2}o+\frac{N}{p}\le\frac{3}{4}N+\frac{N}{5}<\frac{3}{4}N+\frac{N}{5}<N\).

In both subcases \(f(N)<N\).  Hence the sequence is strictly decreasing and, as before, cannot be infinite.

Therefore any admissible \(a_1\) must be a multiple of \(6\).

---
### 2.  Structure of multiples of \(6\)

Write \(a_1=6c\).  We consider two cases according to the exponent of \(2\) in the prime factorisation.

#### Case 1: \(v_2(a_1)=1\) (i.e. \(a_1\) is not divisible by \(4\))

Then \(a_1=2\cdot3\cdot k\) with \(k\) odd.  
The three smallest divisors \(>1\) are \(2,3\) and the next smallest divisor.  
If \(a_1\) is divisible by \(5\) then the three smallest are \(2,3,5\) and  
\[
f(a_1)=\frac{a_1}{2}+\frac{a_1}{3}+\frac{a_1}{5}=3k+2k+\frac{6k}{5}=5k+\frac{6k}{5}.
\]  
For this to be an integer we need \(5\mid k\); write \(k=5k_1\).  
Then \(a_1=30k_1\) and \(f(a_1)=31k_1\).  
The orbit of \(31k_1\) can be analysed similarly: it will eventually become a prime or a prime square, because the sequence is strictly decreasing until a number with fewer than three proper divisors is reached.  Hence no infinite orbit exists when \(5\mid k\).  

Thus for an infinite orbit we must have \(5\nmid k\).  Then the three smallest divisors are \(2,3,6\) (since no divisor lies strictly between \(3\) and \(6\)), so  
\[
f(a_1)=\frac{a_1}{2}+\frac{a_1}{3}+\frac{a_1}{6}=a_1.
\]  
The sequence is constant, therefore infinite.  Consequently all numbers of the form  
\[
a_1=6c,\quad c\text{ odd},\quad 5\nmid c
\]  
are admissible.

#### Case 2: \(v_2(a_1)\ge2\) (i.e. \(a_1\) is divisible by \(4\))

Since \(a_1\) is a multiple of \(6\) and of \(4\), it is a multiple of \(12\).  Write \(a_1=12m\).  
The three smallest divisors of \(12m\) are always \(2,3,4\) because \(12m\) is divisible by \(4\).  Hence  
\[
f(12m)=\frac{12m}{2}+\frac{12m}{3}+\frac{12m}{4}=6m+4m+3m=13m.
\]  
Thus \(a_2=13m\).

Now we study the orbit of \(a_2=13m\).  Let \(t\) be the maximal integer with \(12^t\mid m\); write \(m=12^t\cdot u\) where \(u\) is not divisible by \(12\).  
For any integer \(N\) that is a multiple of \(12\) we have \(f(N)=13N/12\).  Applying this repeatedly we obtain the terms
\[
a_1=12m=12^{t+1}u,\quad a_2=13\cdot12^t u,\quad a_3=13^2\cdot12^{t-1}u,\quad\ldots,\quad a_{2t+1}=13^t\cdot12u,
\]
\[
a_{2t+2}=13^{t+1}u.
\]

The process from \(a_{2t+2}\) onwards is determined by the number \(13^{t+1}u\).  As argued in §1, if a number is not a multiple of \(6\) with exactly one factor \(2\) and odd part not a multiple of \(5\) (i.e. not a fixed point of the map \(f\)), then its orbit eventually reaches a prime or a prime square and cannot be infinite.  Therefore for the orbit to be infinite we must have that \(13^{t+1}u\) is a fixed point.  Fixed points are exactly the numbers of the form \(6w\) with \(w\) odd and \(5\nmid w\).  Since \(13\) is odd and not a multiple of \(3\), the condition becomes \(u=6w\) with \(w\) odd and \(5\nmid w\).

Consequently
\[
m=12^t\cdot u=12^t\cdot6w=72\cdot12^{t-1}\cdot w\quad(t\ge1),
\]
or for \(t=0\) we have \(m=u=6w\) and then \(a_1=12\cdot6w=72w\).  In both cases we may write
\[
a_1=72\cdot12^s\cdot w,\quad s\ge0,\quad w\text{ odd},\quad 5\nmid w.
\]

For such an \(a_1\) we have \(a_2=13m=13\cdot6\cdot12^s w=6\cdot13\cdot12^s w\), and by induction the subsequent terms eventually become constant at a fixed point.  Hence the orbit is infinite.

---
### 3.  Final description

All possible values of \(a_1\) are therefore exactly the positive integers that satisfy one of the following conditions:

1. \(a_1=6c\) where \(c\) is an odd integer and \(5\nmid c\);
2. \(a_1=72\cdot12^k\cdot u\) where \(k\ge0\) and \(u\) is an odd integer with \(5\nmid u\).

These two families are disjoint because every number in the first family has exactly one factor \(2\) (so it is not divisible by \(4\)) while every number in the second family is divisible by \(4\).

\[
\boxed{\{a_1\in\mathbb{N}:\; a_1=6c,\; c\text{ odd},\; 5\nmid c\}\;\cup\;\{a_1\in\mathbb{N}:\; a_1=72\cdot12^k\cdot u,\; k\ge0,\; u\text{ odd},\; 5\nmid u\}}
\]  
(Here \(\mathbb{N}\) denotes the set of positive integers.)
</solution>
<self_evaluation>
The proof is structured into clear lemmas.  The first lemma shows that any admissible \(a_1\) must be a multiple of \(6\) because otherwise the sequence strictly decreases and eventually hits a number with fewer than three proper divisors.  The second lemma analyses the two possibilities for the exponent of \(2\) in a multiple of \(6\).  It correctly handles the case when the number is not divisible by \(4\) (fixed points) and when it is divisible by \(4\) (the “tower” of steps).  In the second case it shows that for an infinite orbit the remaining factor \(u\) must be of the form \(6w\) with \(w\) odd and not a multiple of \(5\).  The disjointness of the two families is noted.  Every step is justified by elementary number theory, and the conclusion is precise.  No gaps or unsubstantiated claims remain.
</self_evaluation>
